Realizing his position was untenable, <a href="/wiki/Abdication_of_Napoleon,_1815" title="Abdication of Napoleon, 1815">he abdicated on 22 June in favour of his son</a>. He left Paris three days later and settled at Josephine's former palace in <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Malmaison" title="Château de Malmaison">Malmaison</a> (on the western bank of the <a href="/wiki/Seine" title="Seine">Seine</a> about 17 kilometres (11&#160;mi) west of Paris). Even as Napoleon travelled to Paris, the Coalition forces swept through France (arriving in the vicinity of Paris on 29 June), with the stated intent of restoring Louis XVIII to the French throne.</p>
<p>When Napoleon heard that Prussian troops had orders to capture him dead or alive, he fled to <a href="/wiki/Rochefort,_Charente-Maritime" title="Rochefort, Charente-Maritime">Rochefort</a>, considering an escape to the United States. British ships were blocking every port. Napoleon demanded asylum from the British <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Lewis_Maitland_(Royal_Navy_officer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick Lewis Maitland (Royal Navy officer)">Captain Frederick Maitland</a> on <a href="/wiki/HMS_Bellerophon_(1786)" title="HMS Bellerophon (1786)">HMS&#160;<i>Bellerophon</i></a> on 15 July 1815.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[181]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exile_on_Saint_Helena">Exile on Saint Helena</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_sainthelene.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Napoleon_sainthelene.jpg/220px-Napoleon_sainthelene.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Napoleon_sainthelene.jpg/330px-Napoleon_sainthelene.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Napoleon_sainthelene.jpg/440px-Napoleon_sainthelene.jpg 2x" data-file-width="758" data-file-height="563" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_sainthelene.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Napoleon on <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena" title="Saint Helena">Saint Helena</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Britain kept Napoleon on the island of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena" title="Saint Helena">Saint Helena</a> in the Atlantic Ocean, 1,870&#160;km (1,162&#160;mi) from the west coast of Africa. Napoleon was moved to <a href="/wiki/Longwood_House" title="Longwood House">Longwood House</a> there in December 1815; it had fallen into disrepair, and the location was damp, windswept and unhealthy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> published articles insinuating the British government was trying to hasten his death, and he often complained of the living conditions in letters to the governor and his custodian, <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Lowe" title="Hudson Lowe">Hudson Lowe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[182]</a></sup></p>
<p>With a small cadre of followers, Napoleon dictated his memoirs and grumbled about conditions. Lowe cut Napoleon's expenditure, ruled that no gifts were allowed if they mentioned his imperial status, and made his supporters sign a guarantee they would stay with the prisoner indefinitely.<sup id="cite_ref-Lowe_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe-188">[183]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:16_Napoleons_exole_St_Helena_June1970.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photo of a front garden and large brown building. French flag on a flagpole next to a small cannon." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/16_Napoleons_exole_St_Helena_June1970.jpg/220px-16_Napoleons_exole_St_Helena_June1970.jpg" width="220" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/16_Napoleons_exole_St_Helena_June1970.jpg/330px-16_Napoleons_exole_St_Helena_June1970.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/16_Napoleons_exole_St_Helena_June1970.jpg/440px-16_Napoleons_exole_St_Helena_June1970.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="454" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:16_Napoleons_exole_St_Helena_June1970.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Longwood_House" title="Longwood House">Longwood House</a>, Saint Helena: site of Napoleon's captivity</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There were rumors of plots and even of his escape, but in reality no serious attempts were made.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[184]</a></sup> For English poet <a href="/wiki/Lord_Byron" title="Lord Byron">Lord Byron</a>, Napoleon was the epitome of the Romantic hero, the persecuted, lonely, and flawed genius.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[185]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Napoleon%27s_death_mask" title="Napoleon's death mask">Napoleon's death mask</a> and <a href="/wiki/Retour_des_cendres" title="Retour des cendres">Retour des cendres</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_Death_Mask.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Napoleon_Death_Mask.jpg/150px-Napoleon_Death_Mask.jpg" width="150" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Napoleon_Death_Mask.jpg/225px-Napoleon_Death_Mask.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Napoleon_Death_Mask.jpg/300px-Napoleon_Death_Mask.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="2500" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_Death_Mask.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bronze death mask of Napoleon I. Modeled in 1821, cast in 1833.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>His personal physician, <a href="/wiki/Barry_O%27Meara" class="mw-redirect" title="Barry O'Meara">Barry O'Meara</a>, warned London that his declining state of health was mainly caused by the harsh treatment. Napoleon confined himself for months on end in his damp and wretched habitation of <i>Longwood</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[186]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 1821, Napoleon's health began to deteriorate rapidly. He reconciled with the Catholic Church. He died on 5 May 1821, after confession, <a href="/wiki/Anointing_of_the_Sick_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Anointing of the Sick (Catholic Church)">Extreme Unction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Viaticum" title="Viaticum">Viaticum</a> in the presence of Father Ange Vignali. His last words were, "France, l'armée, tête d'armée, Joséphine" ("France, army, head of the army, Joséphine").<sup id="cite_ref-McLynn655_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn655-192">[187]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[188]</a></sup></p>
<p>Napoleon's original <a href="/wiki/Death_mask" title="Death mask">death mask</a> was created around 6 May, although it is not clear which doctor created it.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[189]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[note 6]</a></sup> In his will, he had asked to be buried on the banks of the Seine, but the British governor said he should be buried on Saint Helena, in the Valley of the Willows.<sup id="cite_ref-McLynn655_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn655-192">[187]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleone_Bonaparte%27s_Tomb.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Napoleone_Bonaparte%27s_Tomb.jpg/220px-Napoleone_Bonaparte%27s_Tomb.jpg" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Napoleone_Bonaparte%27s_Tomb.jpg/330px-Napoleone_Bonaparte%27s_Tomb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Napoleone_Bonaparte%27s_Tomb.jpg/440px-Napoleone_Bonaparte%27s_Tomb.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4635" data-file-height="3580" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleone_Bonaparte%27s_Tomb.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Napoleon's tomb at <a href="/wiki/Les_Invalides" title="Les Invalides">Les Invalides</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1840, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Philippe_I" title="Louis Philippe I">Louis Philippe I</a> obtained permission from the British to return Napoleon's remains to France. On 15 December 1840, a <a href="/wiki/State_funeral" title="State funeral">state funeral</a> was held. The hearse proceeded from the Arc de Triomphe down the <a href="/wiki/Champs-%C3%89lys%C3%A9es" title="Champs-Élysées">Champs-Élysées</a>, across the <a href="/wiki/Place_de_la_Concorde" title="Place de la Concorde">Place de la Concorde</a> to the <i><a href="/wiki/Les_Invalides" title="Les Invalides">Esplanade des Invalides</a></i> and then to the cupola in St Jérôme's Chapel, where it remained until the tomb designed by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Visconti" title="Louis Visconti">Louis Visconti</a> was completed.</p>
<p>In 1861, Napoleon's remains were entombed in a <a href="/wiki/Porphyry_(geology)" title="Porphyry (geology)">porphyry stone</a> <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus" title="Sarcophagus">sarcophagus</a> in the crypt under the dome at Les Invalides.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[191]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cause_of_death">Cause of death</span></h4>
<p>The cause of his death has been debated. Napoleon's physician, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Carlo_Antommarchi" title="François Carlo Antommarchi">François Carlo Antommarchi</a>, led the autopsy, which found the cause of death to be <a href="/wiki/Stomach_cancer" title="Stomach cancer">stomach cancer</a>. Antommarchi did not sign the official report.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[192]</a></sup> Napoleon's father had died of stomach cancer, although this was seemingly unknown at the time of the autopsy.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[193]</a></sup> Antommarchi found evidence of a stomach ulcer; this was the most convenient explanation for the British, who wanted to avoid criticism over their care of Napoleon.<sup id="cite_ref-McLynn655_192-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn655-192">[187]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_sur_son_lit_de_mort_Horace_Vernet_1826.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Gold-framed portrait painting of a gaunt middle-aged man with receding hair and laurel wreath, lying eyes-closed on white pillow with a white blanket covering to his neck and a gold Jesus cross resting on his chest" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Napoleon_sur_son_lit_de_mort_Horace_Vernet_1826.jpg/220px-Napoleon_sur_son_lit_de_mort_Horace_Vernet_1826.jpg" width="220" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Napoleon_sur_son_lit_de_mort_Horace_Vernet_1826.jpg/330px-Napoleon_sur_son_lit_de_mort_Horace_Vernet_1826.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Napoleon_sur_son_lit_de_mort_Horace_Vernet_1826.jpg/440px-Napoleon_sur_son_lit_de_mort_Horace_Vernet_1826.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1829" data-file-height="1670" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_sur_son_lit_de_mort_Horace_Vernet_1826.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Napoleon on His Death Bed</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Horace_Vernet" title="Horace Vernet">Horace Vernet</a>, 1826</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1955, the diaries of Napoleon's valet, Louis Marchand, were published. His description of Napoleon in the months before his death led <a href="/wiki/Sten_Forshufvud" title="Sten Forshufvud">Sten Forshufvud</a> in a 1961 paper in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> to put forward other causes for his death, including deliberate <a href="/wiki/Arsenic_poisoning" title="Arsenic poisoning">arsenic poisoning</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cullen_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cullen-200">[194]</a></sup> Arsenic was used as a poison during the era because it was undetectable when administered over a long period. Forshufvud, in a 1978 book with <a href="/wiki/Ben_Weider" title="Ben Weider">Ben Weider</a>, noted that Napoleon's body was found to be well preserved when moved in 1840. Arsenic is a strong preservative, and therefore this supported the poisoning hypothesis. Forshufvud and Weider observed that Napoleon had attempted to quench abnormal thirst by drinking large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Orgeat_syrup" title="Orgeat syrup">orgeat syrup</a> that contained cyanide compounds in the almonds used for flavouring.<sup id="cite_ref-Cullen_200-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cullen-200">[194]</a></sup></p>
<p>They maintained that the <a href="/wiki/Potassium_tartrate" title="Potassium tartrate">potassium tartrate</a> used in his treatment prevented his stomach from expelling these compounds and that his thirst was a symptom of the poison. Their hypothesis was that the <a href="/wiki/Calomel" title="Calomel">calomel</a> given to Napoleon became an overdose, which killed him and left extensive <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissue</a> damage behind.<sup id="cite_ref-Cullen_200-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cullen-200">[194]</a></sup> According to a 2007 article, the type of arsenic found in Napoleon's hair shafts was mineral, the most toxic, and according to toxicologist Patrick Kintz, this supported the conclusion that he was murdered.<sup id="cite_ref-cullen156_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cullen156-201">[195]</a></sup></p>
<p>There have been modern studies that have supported the original autopsy finding.<sup id="cite_ref-cullen156_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cullen156-201">[195]</a></sup> In a 2008 study, researchers analysed samples of Napoleon's hair from throughout his life, as well as samples from his family and other contemporaries. All samples had high levels of arsenic, approximately 100 times higher than the current average. According to these researchers, Napoleon's body was already heavily contaminated with arsenic as a boy, and the high arsenic concentration in his hair was not caused by intentional poisoning; people were constantly exposed to arsenic from glues and dyes throughout their lives.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[note 7]</a></sup> Studies published in 2007 and 2008 dismissed evidence of arsenic poisoning, and confirmed evidence of <a href="/wiki/Peptic_ulcer" class="mw-redirect" title="Peptic ulcer">peptic ulcer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gastric_cancer" class="mw-redirect" title="Gastric cancer">gastric cancer</a> as the cause of death.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[197]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_and_the_Catholic_Church" title="Napoleon and the Catholic Church">Napoleon and the Catholic Church</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:France_dioceses_1801.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/France_dioceses_1801.svg/220px-France_dioceses_1801.svg.png" width="220" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/France_dioceses_1801.svg/330px-France_dioceses_1801.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/France_dioceses_1801.svg/440px-France_dioceses_1801.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1345" data-file-height="1412" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:France_dioceses_1801.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Reorganisation of the religious geography: France is divided into 59 <a href="/wiki/Diocese" title="Diocese">dioceses</a> and 10 <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_provinces" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecclesiastical provinces">ecclesiastical provinces</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Napoleon's baptism took place in <a href="/wiki/Ajaccio" title="Ajaccio">Ajaccio</a> on 21 July 1771; he was piously raised as a Catholic but he never developed much faith.<sup id="cite_ref-napoleon.org_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-napoleon.org-205">[198]</a></sup> As an adult, Napoleon was a <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deist</a>. Napoleon's deity was an absent and distant God. However he had a keen appreciation of the power of organized religion in social and political affairs, and paid a great deal of attention to bending it to his purposes. He noted the influence of Catholicism's rituals and splendors.<sup id="cite_ref-napoleon.org_205-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-napoleon.org-205">[198]</a></sup> Napoleon had a <a href="/wiki/Civil_marriage" title="Civil marriage">civil marriage</a> with Joséphine de Beauharnais, without religious ceremony. Napoleon was crowned Emperor on 2 December 1804 at <a href="/wiki/Notre-Dame_de_Paris" title="Notre-Dame de Paris">Notre-Dame de Paris</a> in a ceremony presided over by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_VII" title="Pope Pius VII">Pope Pius VII</a>. On 1 April 1810, Napoleon married the Austrian princess <a href="/wiki/Marie_Louise,_Duchess_of_Parma" title="Marie Louise, Duchess of Parma">Marie Louise</a> in a Catholic ceremony. During his <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Spain_(Napoleonic)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Spain (Napoleonic)">brother's rule in Spain</a>, he abolished the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Inquisition" title="Spanish Inquisition">Spanish Inquisition</a> in 1813. Napoleon was excommunicated by the Catholic Church, but later reconciled with the Church before his death in 1821.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concordat">Concordat</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Concordat_of_1801" title="Concordat of 1801">Concordat of 1801</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FrenchChurchOathConcordat.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/FrenchChurchOathConcordat.jpg/220px-FrenchChurchOathConcordat.jpg" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/FrenchChurchOathConcordat.jpg/330px-FrenchChurchOathConcordat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/FrenchChurchOathConcordat.jpg/440px-FrenchChurchOathConcordat.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1076" data-file-height="849" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FrenchChurchOathConcordat.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Leaders of the Catholic Church taking the civil oath required by <a href="/wiki/Concordat_of_1801" title="Concordat of 1801">the Concordat</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Seeking national reconciliation between revolutionaries and Catholics, the Concordat of 1801 was signed on 15 July 1801 between Napoleon and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_VII" title="Pope Pius VII">Pope Pius VII</a>. It solidified the Roman Catholic Church as the majority church of France and brought back most of its civil status. The hostility of devout Catholics against the state had now largely been resolved. It did not restore the vast church lands and endowments that had been seized during the revolution and sold off. As a part of the Concordat, he presented another set of laws called the <a href="/wiki/Organic_Articles" title="Organic Articles">Organic Articles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[199]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[200]</a></sup></p>
<p>While the Concordat restored much power to the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">papacy</a>, the balance of church–state relations had tilted firmly in Napoleon's favour. He selected the bishops and supervised church finances. Napoleon and the pope both found the Concordat useful. Similar arrangements were made with the Church in territories controlled by Napoleon, especially Italy and Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[201]</a></sup> Now, Napoleon could win favor with the Catholics while also controlling Rome in a political sense. Napoleon said in April 1801, "Skillful conquerors have not got entangled with priests. They can both contain them and use them". French children were issued a catechism that taught them to love and respect Napoleon.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[202]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_emancipation">Religious emancipation</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_and_the_Jews" title="Napoleon and the Jews">Napoleon and the Jews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_and_Protestants" title="Napoleon and Protestants">Napoleon and Protestants</a></div>
<p>Napoleon <a href="/wiki/Jewish_emancipation" title="Jewish emancipation">emancipated Jews</a>, as well as Protestants in Catholic countries and Catholics in Protestant countries, from laws which restricted them to <a href="/wiki/Ghetto" title="Ghetto">ghettos</a>, and he expanded their rights to property, worship, and careers. Despite the anti-semitic reaction to Napoleon's policies from foreign governments and within France, he believed emancipation would benefit France by attracting Jews to the country given the restrictions they faced elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[203]</a></sup></p>
<p>He stated, "I will never accept any proposals that will obligate the Jewish people to leave France, because to me the Jews are the same as any other citizen in our country. It takes weakness to chase them out of the country, but it takes strength to assimilate them".<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[204]</a></sup> He was seen as so favourable to the Jews that the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a> formally condemned him as "<a href="/wiki/Antichrist" title="Antichrist">Antichrist</a> and the Enemy of God".<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[205]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality">Personality</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_visiting_the_Tribunat_(Palais_Royal)_in_1807.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Napoleon_visiting_the_Tribunat_%28Palais_Royal%29_in_1807.jpg/220px-Napoleon_visiting_the_Tribunat_%28Palais_Royal%29_in_1807.jpg" width="220" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Napoleon_visiting_the_Tribunat_%28Palais_Royal%29_in_1807.jpg/330px-Napoleon_visiting_the_Tribunat_%28Palais_Royal%29_in_1807.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4e/Napoleon_visiting_the_Tribunat_%28Palais_Royal%29_in_1807.jpg 2x" data-file-width="435" data-file-height="573" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_visiting_the_Tribunat_(Palais_Royal)_in_1807.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Napoleon visiting the Palais Royal for the opening of the 8th session of the Tribunat in 1807, by <a href="/wiki/Merry-Joseph_Blondel" title="Merry-Joseph Blondel">Merry-Joseph Blondel</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Historians emphasize the strength of the ambition that took Napoleon from an obscure village to command of most of Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[206]</a></sup> George F. E. Rudé stresses his "rare combination of will, intellect and physical vigour".<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[207]</a></sup> At 5&#160;feet 2&#160;inches (1.57&#160;m), he was the average French male but short for an officer.<sup id="cite_ref-Owen_Connelly_2006_7_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Owen_Connelly_2006_7-215">[208]</a></sup> In one-on-one situations he typically had a hypnotic effect on people, seemingly bending the strongest leaders to his will.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[209]</a></sup> He understood military technology, but was not an innovator in that regard.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[210]</a></sup> He was an innovator in using the financial, bureaucratic, and diplomatic resources of France. He could rapidly dictate a series of complex commands to his subordinates, keeping in mind where major units were expected to be at each future point, and like a chess master, "seeing" the best plays moves ahead.<sup id="cite_ref-See_David_Chandler_1975_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-See_David_Chandler_1975-218">[211]</a></sup></p>
<p>Napoleon maintained strict, efficient work habits, prioritizing what needed to be done. He cheated at cards, but repaid the losses; he had to win at everything he attempted.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[212]</a></sup> He kept relays of staff and secretaries at work. Unlike many generals, Napoleon did not examine history to ask what <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander</a> or anyone else did in a similar situation. Critics said he won many battles simply because of luck; Napoleon responded, "Give me lucky generals", aware that "luck" comes to leaders who recognize opportunity, and seize it.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[213]</a></sup> Dwyer states that Napoleon's victories at Austerlitz and Jena in 1805–06 heightened his sense of self-grandiosity, leaving him even more certain of his destiny and invincibility.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[214]</a></sup></p>
<p>In terms of influence on events, it was more than Napoleon's personality that took effect. He reorganized France itself to supply the men and money needed for wars.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[215]</a></sup> He inspired his men—<a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Wellington</a> said his presence on the battlefield was worth 40,000 soldiers, for he inspired confidence from privates to field marshals.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[216]</a></sup> He also unnerved the enemy. At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Auerstadt" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Auerstadt">Battle of Auerstadt</a> in 1806, King <a href="/wiki/Frederick_William_III_of_Prussia" title="Frederick William III of Prussia">Frederick William III of Prussia</a> outnumbered the French by 63,000 to 27,000; however, when he was told, mistakenly, that Napoleon was in command, he ordered a hasty retreat that turned into a rout.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[217]</a></sup> The force of his personality neutralized material difficulties as his soldiers fought with the confidence that with Napoleon in charge they would surely win.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[218]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Image">Image</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_Napoleon" title="Cultural depictions of Napoleon">Cultural depictions of Napoleon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_in_1806.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Napoleon_in_1806.PNG/170px-Napoleon_in_1806.PNG" width="170" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Napoleon_in_1806.PNG/255px-Napoleon_in_1806.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Napoleon_in_1806.PNG/340px-Napoleon_in_1806.PNG 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1740" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_in_1806.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Napoleon is often represented in his green colonel uniform of the <a href="/wiki/Chasseurs_%C3%A0_Cheval_de_la_Garde_Imp%C3%A9riale" title="Chasseurs à Cheval de la Garde Impériale">Chasseur à Cheval of the Imperial Guard</a>, the regiment that often served as his personal escort, with a large <a href="/wiki/Bicorne" title="Bicorne">bicorne</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Hand-in-waistcoat" title="Hand-in-waistcoat">hand-in-waistcoat</a> gesture.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Napoleon has become a worldwide cultural icon who symbolises military genius and political power. <a href="/wiki/Martin_van_Creveld" title="Martin van Creveld">Martin van Creveld</a> described him as "the most competent human being who ever lived".<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[219]</a></sup> Since his death, many towns, streets, ships, and even cartoon characters have been named after him. He has been portrayed in hundreds of films and discussed in hundreds of thousands of books and articles.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[220]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the Napoleonic Wars he was taken seriously by the British press as a dangerous <a href="/wiki/Tyrant" title="Tyrant">tyrant</a>, poised to invade. The British nicknamed him Boney.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A <a href="/wiki/Nursery_rhyme" title="Nursery rhyme">nursery rhyme</a> warned children that Bonaparte ravenously ate naughty people; the "<a href="/wiki/Bogeyman" title="Bogeyman">bogeyman</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[221]</a></sup> At the time of death he was measured as 5&#160;feet 2&#160;inches (1.57&#160;m), which was the average French male height but short for an officer. Some historians believe that the obsolete French feet were used, making him 5&#160;feet 6&#160;inches (1.68&#160;m) with an English yardstick, but he had done his best to make French convert to the metric system, which was adopted already in 1793.<sup id="cite_ref-Owen_Connelly_2006_7_215-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Owen_Connelly_2006_7-215">[208]</a></sup> Napoleon surrounded himself with tall bodyguards and was affectionately nicknamed <i>le petit caporal</i>, reflecting his reported camaraderie with his soldiers rather than his height. In spite of that, the <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_Complex" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon Complex">Napoleon Complex</a> is named after him to describe men who have an inferiority complex and become aggressive due to short stature.</p>
<p>In 1908 <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Adler" title="Alfred Adler">Alfred Adler</a>, a psychologist, cited Napoleon to describe an <a href="/wiki/Inferiority_complex" title="Inferiority complex">inferiority complex</a> in which short people adopt an over-aggressive behaviour to compensate for lack of height; this inspired the term <i><a href="/wiki/Napoleon_complex" title="Napoleon complex">Napoleon complex</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[222]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Stock_character" title="Stock character">stock character</a> of Napoleon is a comically short "petty tyrant" and this has become a cliché in popular culture. He is often portrayed wearing a large <a href="/wiki/Bicorne" title="Bicorne">bicorne</a> hat with a <a href="/wiki/Hand-in-waistcoat" title="Hand-in-waistcoat">hand-in-waistcoat</a> gesture—a reference to the painting produced in 1812 by Jacques-Louis David.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[223]</a></sup></p>
<p>When he became First Consul and later Emperor, Napoleon eschewed his general's uniform and habitually wore the green colonel uniform (non-Hussar) of a colonel of the <a href="/wiki/Chasseurs_%C3%A0_Cheval_de_la_Garde_Imp%C3%A9riale" title="Chasseurs à Cheval de la Garde Impériale">Chasseur à Cheval of the Imperial Guard</a>, the regiment that served as his personal escort many times, with a large <a href="/wiki/Bicorne" title="Bicorne">bicorne</a>. He also habitually wore (usually on Sundays) the blue uniform of a colonel of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Guard_(Napoleon_I)" title="Imperial Guard (Napoleon I)">Imperial Guard</a> Foot Grenadiers (blue with white facings and red cuffs). He also wore his <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9gion_d%27honneur" class="mw-redirect" title="Légion d'honneur">Légion d'honneur</a> star, medal and ribbon, and the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Iron_Crown" title="Order of the Iron Crown">Order of the Iron Crown</a> decorations, white French-style <a href="/wiki/Culotte" class="mw-redirect" title="Culotte">culottes</a> and white stockings. This was in contrast to the complex uniforms with many decorations of his <a href="/wiki/Marshal_of_the_Empire" title="Marshal of the Empire">marshals</a> and those around him.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reforms">Reforms</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Debret_-_Premiere_distribution_des_decorations_de_la_Legion_d%27honneur.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Debret_-_Premiere_distribution_des_decorations_de_la_Legion_d%27honneur.jpg/220px-Debret_-_Premiere_distribution_des_decorations_de_la_Legion_d%27honneur.jpg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Debret_-_Premiere_distribution_des_decorations_de_la_Legion_d%27honneur.jpg/330px-Debret_-_Premiere_distribution_des_decorations_de_la_Legion_d%27honneur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Debret_-_Premiere_distribution_des_decorations_de_la_Legion_d%27honneur.jpg/440px-Debret_-_Premiere_distribution_des_decorations_de_la_Legion_d%27honneur.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1400" data-file-height="1074" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Debret_-_Premiere_distribution_des_decorations_de_la_Legion_d%27honneur.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
First remittance of the Légion d'Honneur, 15 July 1804, at <a href="/wiki/Les_Invalides" title="Les Invalides">Saint-Louis des Invalides</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Debret" title="Jean-Baptiste Debret">Jean-Baptiste Debret</a> (1812).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Napoleon instituted various reforms, such as higher education, a <a href="/wiki/Tax_code" class="mw-redirect" title="Tax code">tax code</a>, road and sewer systems, and established the <a href="/wiki/Banque_de_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Banque de France">Banque de France</a>, the first central bank in French history. He negotiated the <a href="/wiki/Concordat_of_1801" title="Concordat of 1801">Concordat of 1801</a> with the Catholic Church, which sought to reconcile the mostly Catholic population to his regime. It was presented alongside the <a href="/wiki/Organic_Articles" title="Organic Articles">Organic Articles</a>, which regulated public worship in France. He dissolved the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> prior to <a href="/wiki/German_Unification" class="mw-redirect" title="German Unification">German Unification</a> later in the 19th century. The sale of the Louisiana Territory to the United States doubled the size of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[224]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 1802, he instituted the <a href="/wiki/Legion_of_Honour" title="Legion of Honour">Legion of Honour</a>, a substitute for the old royalist decorations and <a href="/wiki/Chivalric_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Chivalric order">orders of chivalry</a>, to encourage civilian and military achievements; the order is still the highest decoration in France.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[225]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Napoleonic_Code">Napoleonic Code</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Code" title="Napoleonic Code">Napoleonic Code</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Code_Civil_1804.png" class="image"><img alt="Page of French writing" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Code_Civil_1804.png/220px-Code_Civil_1804.png" width="220" height="264" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Code_Civil_1804.png/330px-Code_Civil_1804.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Code_Civil_1804.png/440px-Code_Civil_1804.png 2x" data-file-width="636" data-file-height="762" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Code_Civil_1804.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
First page of the 1804 original edition of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_code" title="Civil code">Code Civil</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Napoleon's <a href="/wiki/Civil_code" title="Civil code">set of civil laws</a>, the <i>Code Civil</i>—now often known as the Napoleonic Code—was prepared by committees of legal experts under the supervision of <a href="/wiki/Jean_Jacques_R%C3%A9gis_de_Cambac%C3%A9r%C3%A8s" title="Jean Jacques Régis de Cambacérès">Jean Jacques Régis de Cambacérès</a>, the <i>Second Consul</i>. Napoleon participated actively in the sessions of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_State_(France)" class="mw-redirect" title="Council of State (France)">Council of State</a> that revised the drafts. The development of the code was a fundamental change in the nature of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> legal system with its stress on clearly written and accessible law. Other codes ("<a href="/wiki/Les_cinq_codes" title="Les cinq codes">Les cinq codes</a>") were commissioned by Napoleon to codify criminal and commerce law; a Code of Criminal Instruction was published, which enacted rules of <a href="/wiki/Due_process" title="Due process">due process</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[226]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Napoleonic code was adopted throughout much of Continental Europe, though only in the lands he conquered, and remained in force after Napoleon's defeat. Napoleon said: "My true glory is not to have won forty battles&#160;... Waterloo will erase the memory of so many victories.&#160;... But&#160;... what will live forever, is my Civil Code".<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[227]</a></sup> The Code influences a quarter of the world's jurisdictions such as that of in Continental Europe, the Americas and Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[228]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dieter Langewiesche described the code as a "revolutionary project" which spurred the development of <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeois society</a> in Germany by the extension of the right to own property and an acceleration towards the end of <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a>. Napoleon reorganised what had been the Holy Roman Empire, made up of more than a thousand entities,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (August 2016)">quantify</span></a></i>]</sup> into a more streamlined forty-state <a href="/wiki/Confederation_of_the_Rhine" title="Confederation of the Rhine">Confederation of the Rhine</a>; this provided the basis for the <a href="/wiki/German_Confederation" title="German Confederation">German Confederation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Unification_of_Germany" title="Unification of Germany">unification of Germany</a> in 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[229]</a></sup></p>
<p>The movement toward national unification in Italy was similarly precipitated by Napoleonic rule.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[230]</a></sup> These changes contributed to the development of nationalism and the <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[231]</a></sup></p>
<p>Napoleon implemented a wide array of liberal reforms in France and across Continental Europe, especially in Italy and Germany, as summarized by British historian <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Roberts_(historian)" title="Andrew Roberts (historian)">Andrew Roberts</a>:</p>
<dl>
<dd>The ideas that underpin our modern world–meritocracy, equality before the law, property rights, religious toleration, modern secular education, sound finances, and so on–were championed, consolidated, codified and geographically extended by Napoleon. To them he added a rational and efficient local administration, an end to rural banditry, the encouragement of science and the arts, the abolition of feudalism and the greatest codification of laws since the fall of the Roman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[232]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Napoleon directly overthrew feudal remains in much of western Continental Europe. He liberalised <a href="/wiki/Property_law" title="Property law">property laws</a>, ended <a href="/wiki/Manorialism" title="Manorialism">seigneurial dues</a>, abolished the <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guild</a> of merchants and craftsmen to facilitate entrepreneurship, legalised divorce, closed the <a href="/wiki/Ghetto" title="Ghetto">Jewish ghettos</a> and made Jews equal to everyone else. The <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Inquisition" title="Spanish Inquisition">Inquisition</a> ended as did the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>. The power of church courts and religious authority was sharply reduced and <a href="/wiki/Equality_before_the_law" title="Equality before the law">equality under the law</a> was proclaimed for all men.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[233]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare">Warfare</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_weaponry_and_warfare" title="Napoleonic weaponry and warfare">Napoleonic weaponry and warfare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Military_career_of_Napoleon_Bonaparte" title="Military career of Napoleon Bonaparte">Military career of Napoleon Bonaparte</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_a_Cherbourg_bordercropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photo of a grey and phosphorous-coloured equestrian statue. Napoleon is seated on the horse, which is rearing up, he looks forward with his right hand raised and pointing forward; his left hand holds the reins." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Napoleon_a_Cherbourg_bordercropped.jpg/170px-Napoleon_a_Cherbourg_bordercropped.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Napoleon_a_Cherbourg_bordercropped.jpg/255px-Napoleon_a_Cherbourg_bordercropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Napoleon_a_Cherbourg_bordercropped.jpg/340px-Napoleon_a_Cherbourg_bordercropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="594" data-file-height="794" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_a_Cherbourg_bordercropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue in <a href="/wiki/Cherbourg-Octeville" title="Cherbourg-Octeville">Cherbourg-Octeville</a> unveiled by Napoleon III in 1858. Napoleon I strengthened the town's defences to prevent British naval incursions.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the field of <a href="/wiki/Military_organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Military organisation">military organisation</a>, Napoleon borrowed from previous theorists such as <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Antoine_Hippolyte,_Comte_de_Guibert" title="Jacques Antoine Hippolyte, Comte de Guibert">Jacques Antoine Hippolyte, Comte de Guibert</a>, and from the reforms of preceding French governments, and then developed much of what was already in place. He continued the policy, which emerged from the Revolution, of promotion based primarily on merit.<sup id="cite_ref-Archer_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Archer-241">[234]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Corps" title="Corps">Corps</a> replaced divisions as the largest army units, <a href="/wiki/Self-propelled_artillery" title="Self-propelled artillery">mobile artillery</a> was integrated into reserve batteries, the staff system became more fluid and cavalry returned as an important formation in French military doctrine. These methods are now referred to as essential features of Napoleonic warfare.<sup id="cite_ref-Archer_241-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Archer-241">[234]</a></sup> Though he consolidated the practice of modern <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscription</a> introduced by the Directory, one of the restored monarchy's first acts was to end it.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[235]</a></sup></p>
<p>His opponents learned from Napoleon's innovations. The increased importance of artillery after 1807 stemmed from his creation of a highly mobile artillery force, the growth in artillery numbers, and changes in artillery practices. As a result of these factors, Napoleon, rather than relying on infantry to wear away the enemy's defenses, now could use massed artillery as a spearhead to pound a break in the enemy's line that was then exploited by supporting infantry and cavalry. McConachy rejects the alternative theory that growing reliance on artillery by the French army beginning in 1807 was an outgrowth of the declining quality of the French infantry and, later, France's inferiority in cavalry numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">[236]</a></sup> Weapons and other kinds of military technology remained static through the Revolutionary and Napoleonic eras, but 18th-century <a href="/wiki/Operational_mobility" class="mw-redirect" title="Operational mobility">operational mobility</a> underwent change.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[237]</a></sup></p>
<p>Napoleon's biggest influence was in the conduct of warfare. <a href="/wiki/Antoine-Henri_Jomini" title="Antoine-Henri Jomini">Antoine-Henri Jomini</a> explained Napoleon's methods in a widely used textbook that influenced all European and American armies.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[238]</a></sup> Napoleon was regarded by the influential military theorist <a href="/wiki/Carl_von_Clausewitz" title="Carl von Clausewitz">Carl von Clausewitz</a> as a genius in the operational art of war, and historians rank him as a great military commander.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[239]</a></sup> Wellington, when asked who was the greatest general of the day, answered: "In this age, in past ages, in any age, Napoleon".<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">[240]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under Napoleon, a new emphasis towards the destruction, not just outmanoeuvring, of enemy armies emerged. Invasions of enemy territory occurred over broader fronts which made wars costlier and more decisive. The political effect of war increased; defeat for a European power meant more than the loss of isolated enclaves. Near-<a href="/wiki/Carthaginian_peace" title="Carthaginian peace">Carthaginian peaces</a> intertwined whole national efforts, intensifying the Revolutionary phenomenon of total war.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[241]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metric_system">Metric system</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 200px;">
<div style="width: 200px; height: 200px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -5px; left: -6px; width: 410px"><a href="/wiki/File:France_1803-04-A_20_Francs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Depicted as First Consul on the 1803 20 gold Napoléon gold coin." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/France_1803-04-A_20_Francs.jpg/410px-France_1803-04-A_20_Francs.jpg" width="410" height="212" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/France_1803-04-A_20_Francs.jpg/615px-France_1803-04-A_20_Francs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/France_1803-04-A_20_Francs.jpg/820px-France_1803-04-A_20_Francs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4827" data-file-height="2500" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:France_1803-04-A_20_Francs.jpg" title="File:France 1803-04-A 20 Francs.jpg">File:France 1803-04-A 20 Francs.jpg</a></div>
Depicted as <a href="/wiki/French_Consulate" title="French Consulate">First Consul</a> on the 1803 20 gold <a href="/wiki/Napol%C3%A9on_(coin)" title="Napoléon (coin)">Napoléon</a> gold coin.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_metric_system" title="History of the metric system">History of the metric system</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesures_usuelles" title="Mesures usuelles">Mesures usuelles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Units_of_measurement_in_France" title="Units of measurement in France">Units of measurement in France</a></div>
<p>The official introduction of the metric system in September 1799 was unpopular in large sections of French society. Napoleon's rule greatly aided adoption of the new standard not only across France but also across the French <a href="/wiki/Sphere_of_influence" title="Sphere of influence">sphere of influence</a>. Napoleon took a retrograde step in 1812 when he passed legislation to introduce the <i><a href="/wiki/Mesures_usuelles" title="Mesures usuelles">mesures usuelles</a></i> (traditional units of measurement) for retail trade<sup id="cite_ref-H.26H_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H.26H-249">[242]</a></sup>—a system of measure that resembled the pre-revolutionary units but were based on the kilogram and the metre; for example the <i>livre metrique</i> (metric pound) was 500&#160;g<sup id="cite_ref-historique_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historique-250">[243]</a></sup> instead of 489.5 g—the value of the <i>livre du roi</i> (the king's pound).<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">[244]</a></sup> Other units of measure were rounded in a similar manner prior to the definitive introduction of the metric system across parts of Europe in the middle of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[245]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3>
<p>Napoleon's educational reforms laid the foundation of a modern system of education in France and throughout much of Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[246]</a></sup> Napoleon synthesized the best academic elements from the <i>Ancien Régime</i>, <a href="/wiki/The_Enlightenment" class="mw-redirect" title="The Enlightenment">The Enlightenment</a>, and the Revolution, with the aim of establishing a stable, well-educated and prosperous society. He made French the only official language. He left some primary education in the hands of religious orders, but he offered public support to secondary education. Napoleon founded a number of state secondary schools (<i><a href="/wiki/Secondary_education_in_France#Lyc.C3.A9e" title="Secondary education in France">lycées</a></i>) designed to produce a standardized education that was uniform across France. All students were taught the sciences along with modern and classical languages. Unlike the system during the <i>Ancien Régime</i>, religious topics did not dominate the curriculum, although they were present with the teachers from the clergy. Napoleon hoped to use religion to produce social stability.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[247]</a></sup> He gave special attention to the advanced centers, such as the École Polytechnique, that provided both military expertise and state-of-the-art research in science.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[248]</a></sup> Napoleon made some of the first efforts at establishing a system of secular and public education.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2016)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> The system featured scholarships and strict discipline, with the result being a French educational system that outperformed its European counterparts, many of which borrowed from the French system.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[249]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Memory_and_evaluation">Memory and evaluation</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Francisco_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_Los_fusilamientos_del_tres_de_mayo_-_1814.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Francisco_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_Los_fusilamientos_del_tres_de_mayo_-_1814.jpg/220px-Francisco_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_Los_fusilamientos_del_tres_de_mayo_-_1814.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Francisco_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_Los_fusilamientos_del_tres_de_mayo_-_1814.jpg/330px-Francisco_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_Los_fusilamientos_del_tres_de_mayo_-_1814.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Francisco_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_Los_fusilamientos_del_tres_de_mayo_-_1814.jpg/440px-Francisco_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_Los_fusilamientos_del_tres_de_mayo_-_1814.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3051" data-file-height="2328" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Francisco_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_Los_fusilamientos_del_tres_de_mayo_-_1814.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/The_Third_of_May_1808" title="The Third of May 1808">The Third of May 1808</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Goya" title="Francisco Goya">Francisco Goya</a>, showing Spanish resisters being executed by Napoleon's troops.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the political realm, historians debate whether Napoleon was "an enlightened despot who laid the foundations of modern Europe or, instead, a megalomaniac who wrought greater misery than any man before the coming of Hitler".<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[250]</a></sup> Many historians have concluded that he had grandiose foreign policy ambitions. The Continental powers as late as 1808 were willing to give him nearly all of his gains and titles, but some scholars maintain he was overly aggressive and pushed for too much, until his empire collapsed.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">[251]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[252]</a></sup></p>
<p>Napoleon ended lawlessness and disorder in post-Revolutionary France.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[253]</a></sup> He was considered a tyrant and <a href="/wiki/Usurper" title="Usurper">usurper</a> by his opponents.<sup id="cite_ref-M666_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M666-261">[254]</a></sup> His critics<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (August 2016)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> charge that he was not troubled when faced with the prospect of war and death for thousands, turned his search for undisputed rule into a series of conflicts throughout Europe and ignored treaties and conventions alike. His role in the <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Revolution" title="Haitian Revolution">Haitian Revolution</a> and decision to reinstate slavery in France's overseas colonies are controversial and affect his reputation.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">[255]</a></sup></p>
<p>Napoleon institutionalised plunder of conquered territories: French museums contain art stolen by Napoleon's forces from across Europe. Artefacts were brought to the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre" class="mw-redirect" title="Musée du Louvre">Musée du Louvre</a> for a grand central museum; his example would later serve as inspiration for more notorious imitators.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[256]</a></sup> He was compared to <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> most famously by the historian <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Geyl" title="Pieter Geyl">Pieter Geyl</a> in 1947<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[257]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Claude_Ribbe" title="Claude Ribbe">Claude Ribbe</a> in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[258]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_G._Chandler" title="David G. Chandler">David G. Chandler</a>, a foremost historian of Napoleonic warfare, wrote in 1973 that, "Nothing could be more degrading to the former [Napoleon] and more flattering to the latter [Hitler]. The comparison is odious. On the whole Napoleon was inspired by a noble dream, wholly dissimilar from Hitler's... Napoleon left great and lasting testimonies to his genius—in codes of law and national identities which survive to the present day. Adolf Hitler left nothing but destruction".<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[259]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics argue Napoleon's true legacy must reflect the loss of status for France and needless deaths brought by his rule: historian <a href="/wiki/Victor_Davis_Hanson" title="Victor Davis Hanson">Victor Davis Hanson</a> writes, "After all, the military record is unquestioned—17 years of wars, perhaps <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars_casualties" title="Napoleonic Wars casualties">six million Europeans dead</a>, France bankrupt, her overseas colonies lost".<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[260]</a></sup> McLynn states that, "He can be viewed as the man who set back European economic life for a generation by the dislocating impact of his wars".<sup id="cite_ref-M666_261-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M666-261">[254]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vincent_Cronin" title="Vincent Cronin">Vincent Cronin</a> replies that such criticism relies on the flawed premise that Napoleon was responsible for the wars which bear his name, when in fact France was the victim of a series of coalitions which aimed to destroy the ideals of the Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-c342_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-c342-268">[261]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Propaganda_and_memory">Propaganda and memory</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_propaganda" title="Napoleonic propaganda">Napoleonic propaganda</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:428px;max-width:428px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Jacques_Louis_David_-_Bonaparte_franchissant_le_Grand_Saint-Bernard,_20_mai_1800_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Jacques_Louis_David_-_Bonaparte_franchissant_le_Grand_Saint-Bernard%2C_20_mai_1800_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Jacques_Louis_David_-_Bonaparte_franchissant_le_Grand_Saint-Bernard%2C_20_mai_1800_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="260" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Jacques_Louis_David_-_Bonaparte_franchissant_le_Grand_Saint-Bernard%2C_20_mai_1800_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Jacques_Louis_David_-_Bonaparte_franchissant_le_Grand_Saint-Bernard%2C_20_mai_1800_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Jacques_Louis_David_-_Bonaparte_franchissant_le_Grand_Saint-Bernard%2C_20_mai_1800_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Jacques_Louis_David_-_Bonaparte_franchissant_le_Grand_Saint-Bernard%2C_20_mai_1800_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3972" data-file-height="4695" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i><a href="/wiki/Napoleon_Crossing_the_Alps" title="Napoleon Crossing the Alps">Napoleon Crossing the Alps</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">romantic</a> version by <a href="/wiki/Jacques-Louis_David" title="Jacques-Louis David">Jacques-Louis David</a> in 1805</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Paul_Delaroche_-_Napoleon_Crossing_the_Alps_-_Google_Art_Project_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Paul_Delaroche_-_Napoleon_Crossing_the_Alps_-_Google_Art_Project_2.jpg/200px-Paul_Delaroche_-_Napoleon_Crossing_the_Alps_-_Google_Art_Project_2.jpg" width="200" height="257" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Paul_Delaroche_-_Napoleon_Crossing_the_Alps_-_Google_Art_Project_2.jpg/300px-Paul_Delaroche_-_Napoleon_Crossing_the_Alps_-_Google_Art_Project_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Paul_Delaroche_-_Napoleon_Crossing_the_Alps_-_Google_Art_Project_2.jpg/400px-Paul_Delaroche_-_Napoleon_Crossing_the_Alps_-_Google_Art_Project_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1977" data-file-height="2545" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i><a href="/wiki/Bonaparte_Crossing_the_Alps" title="Bonaparte Crossing the Alps">Bonaparte Crossing the Alps</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Realism_(art_movement)" title="Realism (art movement)">realist</a> version by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Delaroche" title="Paul Delaroche">Paul Delaroche</a> in 1848</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Napoleon's use of propaganda contributed to his rise to power, legitimated his régime, and established his image for posterity. Strict censorship, controlling aspects of the press, books, theater, and art, was part of his propaganda scheme, aimed at portraying him as bringing desperately wanted peace and stability to France. The propagandistic rhetoric changed in relation to events and to the atmosphere of Napoleon's reign, focusing first on his role as a general in the army and identification as a soldier, and moving to his role as emperor and a civil leader. Specifically targeting his civilian audience, Napoleon fostered a relationship with the contemporary art community, taking an active role in commissioning and controlling different forms of art production to suit his propaganda goals.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">[262]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hazareesingh (2004) explores how Napoleon's image and memory are best understood. They played a key role in collective political defiance of the Bourbon restoration monarchy in 1815–1830. People from different walks of life and areas of France, particularly Napoleonic veterans, drew on the Napoleonic legacy and its connections with the ideals of the 1789 revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-270">[263]</a></sup></p>
<p>Widespread rumors of Napoleon's return from St. Helena and Napoleon as an inspiration for patriotism, individual and collective liberties, and political mobilization manifested themselves in seditious materials, displaying the tricolor and rosettes. There were also subversive activities celebrating anniversaries of Napoleon's life and reign and disrupting royal celebrations—they demonstrated the prevailing and successful goal of the varied supporters of Napoleon to constantly destabilize the Bourbon regime.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_270-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-270">[263]</a></sup></p>
<p>Datta (2005) shows that, following the collapse of militaristic <a href="/wiki/Boulangism" class="mw-redirect" title="Boulangism">Boulangism</a> in the late 1880s, the Napoleonic legend was divorced from party politics and revived in popular culture. Concentrating on two plays and two novels from the period—<a href="/wiki/Victorien_Sardou" title="Victorien Sardou">Victorien Sardou</a>'s <i>Madame Sans-Gêne</i> (1893), <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Barr%C3%A8s" title="Maurice Barrès">Maurice Barrès</a>'s <i>Les Déracinés</i> (1897), <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Rostand" title="Edmond Rostand">Edmond Rostand</a>'s <i>L'Aiglon</i> (1900), and <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_de_Lorde" title="André de Lorde">André de Lorde</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sibylle_Gabrielle_Marie_Antoinette_Riqueti_de_Mirabeau" class="mw-redirect" title="Sibylle Gabrielle Marie Antoinette Riqueti de Mirabeau">Gyp</a>'s <i>Napoléonette</i> (1913)—Datta examines how writers and critics of the <i><a href="/wiki/Belle_%C3%89poque" title="Belle Époque">Belle Époque</a></i> exploited the Napoleonic legend for diverse political and cultural ends.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-271">[264]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reduced to a minor character, the new fictional Napoleon became not a world historical figure but an intimate one, fashioned by individuals' needs and consumed as popular entertainment. In their attempts to represent the emperor as a figure of national unity, proponents and detractors of the Third Republic used the legend as a vehicle for exploring anxieties about gender and fears about the processes of democratization that accompanied this new era of mass politics and culture.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_271-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-271">[264]</a></sup></p>
<p>International Napoleonic Congresses take place regularly, with participation by members of the French and American military, French politicians and scholars from different countries.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">[265]</a></sup> In January 2012, the <a href="/wiki/Mayor_(France)" title="Mayor (France)">mayor</a> of <a href="/wiki/Montereau-Fault-Yonne" title="Montereau-Fault-Yonne">Montereau-Fault-Yonne</a>, near Paris—the site of a <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Montereau" title="Battle of Montereau">late victory</a> of Napoleon—proposed development of <a href="/wiki/Napoleonland" title="Napoleonland">Napoleon's Bivouac</a>, a commemorative theme park at a projected cost of 200 million euros.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">[266]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Long-term_influence_outside_France">Long-term influence outside France</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Influence_of_the_French_Revolution" title="Influence of the French Revolution">Influence of the French Revolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_on_the_Capitol.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Napoleon_on_the_Capitol.jpg/170px-Napoleon_on_the_Capitol.jpg" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Napoleon_on_the_Capitol.jpg/255px-Napoleon_on_the_Capitol.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/25/Napoleon_on_the_Capitol.jpg 2x" data-file-width="319" data-file-height="400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Napoleon_on_the_Capitol.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Bas-reliefs_in_the_chamber_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" class="extiw" title="commons:Bas-reliefs in the chamber of the United States House of Representatives">Bas-relief</a> of Napoleon I in the chamber of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Napoleon was responsible for spreading the values of the French Revolution to other countries, especially in legal reform and the abolition of serfdom.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">[267]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the fall of Napoleon, not only was the Napoleonic Code retained by conquered countries including the Netherlands, Belgium, parts of Italy and Germany, but has been used as the basis of certain parts of law outside Europe including the Dominican Republic, the US state of Louisiana and the Canadian province of Quebec.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">[268]</a></sup> The memory of Napoleon in Poland is favorable, for his support for independence and opposition to Russia, his legal code, the abolition of serfdom, and the introduction of modern middle class bureaucracies.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">[269]</a></sup></p>
<p>Napoleon could be considered one of the founders of modern Germany. After dissolving the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, he reduced the number of German states from 300 to less than 50, prior to the <a href="/wiki/Unification_of_Germany" title="Unification of Germany">German Unification</a>. A byproduct of the French occupation was a strong development in <a href="/wiki/German_nationalism" title="German nationalism">German nationalism</a>. Napoleon also significantly aided the United States when he agreed to <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">sell</a> the territory of Louisiana for 15 million dollars during the presidency of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>. That territory almost doubled the size of the United States, adding the equivalent of 13 states to the Union.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">[270]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriages_and_children">Marriages and children</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;max-width:352px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:189px;max-width:189px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Baron_Fran%C3%A7ois_G%C3%A9rard_-_Jos%C3%A9phine_in_coronation_costume_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Napoleon's first wife, Joséphine, Empress of the French, painted by François Gérard, 1801" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Baron_Fran%C3%A7ois_G%C3%A9rard_-_Jos%C3%A9phine_in_coronation_costume_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/187px-Baron_Fran%C3%A7ois_G%C3%A9rard_-_Jos%C3%A9phine_in_coronation_costume_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="187" height="250" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Baron_Fran%C3%A7ois_G%C3%A9rard_-_Jos%C3%A9phine_in_coronation_costume_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/281px-Baron_Fran%C3%A7ois_G%C3%A9rard_-_Jos%C3%A9phine_in_coronation_costume_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Baron_Fran%C3%A7ois_G%C3%A9rard_-_Jos%C3%A9phine_in_coronation_costume_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/374px-Baron_Fran%C3%A7ois_G%C3%A9rard_-_Jos%C3%A9phine_in_coronation_costume_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4097" data-file-height="5476" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Napoleon's first wife, <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9phine_de_Beauharnais" title="Joséphine de Beauharnais">Joséphine</a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:159px;max-width:159px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Jean-Baptiste_Isabey_003.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Empress Marie-Louise and the King of Rome, by Joseph Franque, 1812." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Jean-Baptiste_Isabey_003.jpg/157px-Jean-Baptiste_Isabey_003.jpg" width="157" height="250" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Jean-Baptiste_Isabey_003.jpg/236px-Jean-Baptiste_Isabey_003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Jean-Baptiste_Isabey_003.jpg/314px-Jean-Baptiste_Isabey_003.jpg 2x" data-file-width="483" data-file-height="768" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Napoleon's second wife, <a href="/wiki/Marie_Louise,_Duchess_of_Parma" title="Marie Louise, Duchess of Parma">Marie-Louise</a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Napoleon married <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9phine_de_Beauharnais" title="Joséphine de Beauharnais">Joséphine de Beauharnais</a> in 1796, when he was 26; she was a 32-year-old widow whose first husband had been executed during the Revolution. Until she met Bonaparte, she had been known as "Rose", a name which he disliked. He called her "Joséphine" instead, and she went by this name henceforth. Bonaparte often sent her love letters while on his campaigns.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">[271]</a></sup> He formally adopted her son <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_de_Beauharnais" title="Eugène de Beauharnais">Eugène</a> and cousin <a href="/wiki/St%C3%A9phanie_de_Beauharnais" title="Stéphanie de Beauharnais">Stéphanie</a> and arranged dynastic marriages for them. Joséphine had her daughter <a href="/wiki/Hortense_de_Beauharnais" title="Hortense de Beauharnais">Hortense</a> marry Napoleon's brother <a href="/wiki/Louis_Bonaparte" title="Louis Bonaparte">Louis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">[272]</a></sup></p>
<p>Joséphine had lovers, such as lieutenant Hippolyte Charles, during Napoleon's Italian campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">[273]</a></sup> Napoleon learnt of that affair and a letter he wrote about it was intercepted by the British and published widely, to embarrass Napoleon. Napoleon had his own affairs too: during the Egyptian campaign he took Pauline Bellisle Foures, the wife of a junior officer, as his mistress. She became known as "Cleopatra".<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">[274]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">[note 8]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Plate_showing_statues_of_Amenhotep_III_at_Luxor,_Egypt._Commissioned_by_Napoleon_as_a_present_to_Josephine_but_she_rejected_it._From_France._The_Victoria_and_Albert_Museum,_London.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Plate_showing_statues_of_Amenhotep_III_at_Luxor%2C_Egypt._Commissioned_by_Napoleon_as_a_present_to_Josephine_but_she_rejected_it._From_France._The_Victoria_and_Albert_Museum%2C_London.jpg/170px-thumbnail.jpg" width="170" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Plate_showing_statues_of_Amenhotep_III_at_Luxor%2C_Egypt._Commissioned_by_Napoleon_as_a_present_to_Josephine_but_she_rejected_it._From_France._The_Victoria_and_Albert_Museum%2C_London.jpg/255px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Plate_showing_statues_of_Amenhotep_III_at_Luxor%2C_Egypt._Commissioned_by_Napoleon_as_a_present_to_Josephine_but_she_rejected_it._From_France._The_Victoria_and_Albert_Museum%2C_London.jpg/340px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4115" data-file-height="3731" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Plate_showing_statues_of_Amenhotep_III_at_Luxor,_Egypt._Commissioned_by_Napoleon_as_a_present_to_Josephine_but_she_rejected_it._From_France._The_Victoria_and_Albert_Museum,_London.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Plate showing statues of Amenhotep III at Luxor, Egypt. Commissioned by Napoleon as a present to Josephine but she rejected it. From France. The Victoria and Albert Museum, London</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While Napoleon's mistresses had children by him, Joséphine did not produce an heir, possibly because of either the stresses of her imprisonment during the <a href="/wiki/Reign_of_Terror" title="Reign of Terror">Reign of Terror</a> or an abortion she may have had in her twenties.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">[276]</a></sup> Napoleon chose divorce so he could remarry in search of an heir. Despite his divorce from Josephine, Napoleon showed his dedication to her for the rest of his life. When he heard the news of her death while on exile in Elba, he locked himself in his room and would not come out for two full days.<sup id="cite_ref-pbs.org_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-181">[176]</a></sup> Her name would also be his final word on his deathbed in 1821.</p>
<p>In March 1810, he married the 19-year old <a href="/wiki/Marie_Louise,_Duchess_of_Parma" title="Marie Louise, Duchess of Parma">Marie Louise</a>, Archduchess of Austria, and a great niece of <a href="/wiki/Marie_Antoinette" title="Marie Antoinette">Marie Antoinette</a> by <a href="/wiki/Proxy_marriage" title="Proxy marriage">proxy</a>; thus he had married into a <a href="/wiki/Family_tree_of_the_German_monarchs" title="Family tree of the German monarchs">German royal and imperial family</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-McLynn_1998.2C_p.663_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn_1998.2C_p.663-285">[277]</a></sup> Louise was less than happy with the arrangement, at least at first, stating "Just to see the man would be the worst form of torture". Her great-aunt had been executed in France, while Napoleon had fought numerous campaigns against Austria all throughout his military career. However, she seemed to warm up to him over time.
